1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`


## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

# Rust Edition 2018 to 2021 Import Path Migration

## Your Task

Find all Rust source files in rust-lang/rust (standard library and core) that implement edition-specific import path handling: the edition-based macro_use difference, the prelude v1 vs v2 module exports, and the resolver behavior changes between edition 2018 and 2021.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the migration domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7, sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99.


## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{
  "files": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go"}
  ],
  "symbols": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "SymbolName"}
  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],
  "text": "Narrative explanation of your findings, citing repos and file paths."
}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

## Files Implementing Edition-Specific Import Path Handling

### Standard Library Prelude Modules
- **`library/core/src/prelude/mod.rs`** — Defines `rust_2015`, `rust_2018`, `rust_2021`, `rust_2024` prelude modules. The `rust_2021` module is the key change: it adds `TryFrom`, `TryInto`, and `FromIterator` on top of v1.
- **`library/core/src/prelude/v1.rs`** — Base v1 prelude re-exported by 2015/2018 editions.
- **`library/std/src/prelude/mod.rs`** — Std-layer edition-specific preludes.
- **`library/std/src/prelude/v1.rs`** — Std base v1 prelude.

### Compiler: Edition-Specific Prelude Injection
- **`compiler/rustc_builtin_macros/src/standard_library_imports.rs`** — The `inject()` function selects the edition-appropriate prelude path (`rust_2015`/`rust_2018`/`rust_2021`) and changes `macro_use` hygiene span between 2015 and 2018+. Also adds `::` prefix (PathRoot) only for 2015.

### Compiler: Resolver Behavior Changes
- **`compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs`** — Defines `macro_use_prelude`, `ExternPrelude`, `ModuleAndExternPrelude`.
- **`compiler/rustc_resolve/src/ident.rs`** — Core resolver: `::foo` paths resolve to `ExternPrelude` in 2018+, to crate root in 2015. `MacroUsePrelude` scope handling.
- **`compiler/rustc_resolve/src/build_reduced_graph.rs`** — `#[macro_use]` processing, 2018 relative-path visibility restrictions.
- **`compiler/rustc_resolve/src/diagnostics.rs`** — Notes about Edition 2021 prelude additions.
- **`compiler/rustc_resolve/src/macros.rs`** — Macro scope resolution ordering.

### Compiler: Prelude Collision Lints
- **`compiler/rustc_hir_typeck/src/method/prelude_edition_lints.rs`** — `lint_edition_dependent_dot_call()` for `rust_2021_prelude_collisions`

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: //! # The Rust Prelude\\n2: //!\\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\\n7: //!\\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\\n11: //! program.\\n12: //!\\n13: //! # Other preludes\\n14: //!\\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\\n21: //!\\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\\n25: //!\\n26: //! # Prelude contents\\n27: //!\\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\\n33: //!\\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\\n40: //!   dropping a value.\\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\\n42: //!   a type or value.\\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\\n48: //!   borrowed type.\\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\\n58: //!   iterators of various\\n59: //!   kinds.\\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\\n65: //!   exported as well.\\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\\n68: //!\\n69: //! However, if\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n7: //!\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n11: //! program.\n12: //!\n13: //! # Other preludes\n14: //!\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\n19: //!\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n21: //!\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n25: //!\n26: //! # Prelude contents\n27: //!\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\n33: //!\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n40: //!   dropping a value.\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n42: //!   a type or value.\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n48: //!   borrowed type.\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n58: //!   iterators of various\n59: //!   kinds.\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\n65: //!   exported as well.\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\n68: //!\n69: //! The prelude used in Rust 2021, [`std::prelude::rust_2021`], includes all of the above,\n70: //! and in addition re-exports:\n71: //!\n72: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[TryFrom], [TryInto]}</code>.\n73: //! * <code>[std::iter]::[FromIterator]</code>.\n74: //!\n75: //! Contains marker traits, ops, collections, and conversion traits but NOT `TryFrom`, `TryInto`, or `FromIterator`.\\n\\n### `library/std/src/prelude/mod.rs`\\nThe std-layer prelude: `rust_2015` and `rust_2018` re-export `super::v1::*`; `rust_2021` additionally re-exports `core::prelude::rust_2021::*` (which adds `TryFrom`, `TryInto`, `FromIterator`).\\n\\n### `library/std/src/prelude/v1.rs`\\nThe std base v1 prelude, including built-in macros and std-specific types re-exported from `core::prelude::v1`.\\n\\n## Compiler: Injection of Edition-Specific Preludes\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_builtin_macros/src/standard_library_imports.rs`\\nThe `inject()` function is the mechanism that inserts the edition-specific prelude import at the beginning of every crate. It:\\n- Emits `#[macro_use] extern crate std` (or `core`) with an `ident_span` that is the def-site span for edition >= 2018 (to make it hygienic), or the call-site span for 2015.\\n- Emits a `#[prelude_import] use ::std::prelude::rust_2015::*` (with absolute `::` root for 2015) or `use std::prelude::rust_2018::*` (no leading `::`) for 2018+.\\n- Selects the edition-appropriate module: `Edition2015 => sym::rust_2015`, `Edition2018 => sym::rust_2018`, `Edition2021 => sym::rust_2021`, etc.\\n\\n## Compiler: Resolver Behavior Changes\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs`\\nDefines the `macro_use_prelude` field (`FxIndexMap<Symbol, NameBinding>`) populated by `#[macro_use]` extern crates. Also defines `ModuleOrUniformRoot::ExternPrelude` (used for `::foo` paths in edition 2018+) and `ModuleAndExternPrelude` (for `::foo` from 2015 edition macros used in 2018+ crates).\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/ident.rs`\\nCore name resolution with edition-specific behavior:\\n- `kw::PathRoot` + `ident.span.at_least_rust_2018()` \u2192 resolves to `ExternPrelude` (2018+ behavior where `::foo` refers to external crates)\\n- `kw::PathRoot` + `ident.span.is_rust_2015()` + session is 2018+ \u2192 uses `ModuleAndExternPrelude` (cross-edition macro hygiene)\\n- `Scope::MacroUsePrelude` searches `macro_use_prelude` for macros imported via `#[macro_use]`\\n- Edition 2015 gets separate `rust_2015` flag controlling macro scope resolution\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/build_reduced_graph.rs`\\nBuilds the reduced module graph. Handles `#[macro_use]` on extern crates (adding macros to `macro_use_prelude`). Enforces that visibility paths in 2018+ editions cannot be module-relative (only crate-relative via `crate::` or absolute `::`).\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/diagnostics.rs`\\nEdition-specific error messages: notes that `TryFrom`, `TryInto`, `FromIterator` are in the prelude starting Edition 2021, explains that `::foo` means extern prelude in 2018+, offers `crate::` prefix suggestions for 2018+.\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/macros.rs`\\nMacro name resolution, including scope ordering where prelude macros (from `macro_use_prelude` and std prelude) come after `macro_rules!` scopes.\\n\\n## Compiler: Prelude Collision Lints\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_hir_typeck/src/method/prelude_edition_lints.rs`\\nImplements `lint_edition_dependent_dot_call()`, the check that warns when a method call (e.g., `.try_into()`) will become ambiguous in Rust 2021 because `TryInto` is added to the prelude. Fires `RUST_2021_PRELUDE_COLLISIONS` or `RUST_2024_PRELUDE_COLLISIONS`.\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_hir_typeck/src/method/suggest.rs`\\nMethod resolution suggestions include a note about TryInto/TryFrom/FromIterator being added to the prelude in Edition 2021.\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_lint_defs/src/builtin.rs`\\nDeclares `rust_2021_prelude_collisions` and `rust_2024_prelude_collisions` lints with edition-error future incompatibility references.\\n\\n## Edition Abstraction\\n\\n### `compiler/rustc_span/src/edition.rs`\\nThe `Edition` enum with `is_rust_2015()`, `at_least_rust_2018()`, `at_least_rust_2021()`, `at_least_rust_2024()` methods. Used pervasively throughout the compiler to branch on edition-specific behavior.\"\n}\n",
